<tr>
<th scope="row">Signature</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg" class="image" title="John Quincy Adams's signature"><img alt="Cursive signature in ink" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg/128px-John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg.png" width="128" height="24" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg/192px-John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg/256px-John_Quincy_Adams_Signature.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="294" data-file-height="56" /></a></td>

</table>
<p><b>John Quincy Adams</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="noexcerpt"><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/07/John_Quincy_Adams_pron.ogg" title="Listen"><img alt="Listen" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/11px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/17px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/22px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="11" data-file-height="11" /></a><sup><span class="IPA" style="color:#00e;font:bold 80% sans-serif;padding:0 .1em"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_pron.ogg" title="File:John Quincy Adams pron.ogg">i</a></span></sup></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[a]</a></sup> July 11, 1767&#160;– February 23, 1848) was an American statesman who served as a diplomat, United States Senator, member of the House of Representatives, and was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">sixth President of the United States</a> from 1825 to 1829. He was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Party" title="National Republican Party">National Republican</a>, and later the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Masonic_Party" title="Anti-Masonic Party">Anti-Masonic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig</a> parties. He was the son of President <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abigail_Adams" title="Abigail Adams">Abigail Adams</a> and thus contributed to the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Adams_political_family" title="Adams political family">Adams political family</a>.</p>
<p>Adams shaped U.S. foreign policy using his ardently nationalist commitment to <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">U.S. republican values</a>. As a diplomat, Adams played an important role in negotiating key treaties, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ghent" title="Treaty of Ghent">Treaty of Ghent</a>, which ended the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>. As <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>, he <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_1818" title="Treaty of 1818">negotiated with Britain</a> over the United States' northern border with Canada, negotiated with Spain the <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">annexation of Florida</a>, and drafted the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a>. Historians generally concur that he was one of the greatest diplomats and secretaries of state in American history.<sup id="cite_ref-Bemis_1981_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bemis_1981-3">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> In his biography, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Flagg_Bemis" title="Samuel Flagg Bemis">Samuel Flagg Bemis</a> argues that Adams was able to "gather together, formulate, and practice the fundamentals of American foreign-policy – self-determination, independence, noncolonization, nonintervention, nonentanglement in European politics, Freedom of the Seas, [and] freedom of commerce."<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adams was elected president in a close and controversial four-way contest in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1824" title="United States presidential election, 1824">1824</a>. As president he sought to <a href="/wiki/American_System_(economic_plan)" title="American System (economic plan)">modernize the American economy</a> and promote education. Adams enacted a part of his agenda and paid off much of the <a href="/wiki/National_debt" class="mw-redirect" title="National debt">national debt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> However he was stymied time and again by a Congress controlled by opponents, and his lack of patronage networks helped politicians sabotage him. He lost his <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1828" title="United States presidential election, 1828">1828 bid for re-election</a> to <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>. He has been portrayed by recent historians as an exemplar and moral leader during an era of modernization, when new modes of communication spread messages of religious revival, social reform, and party politics, and improved transportation moved goods, money, and people more rapidly.<sup id="cite_ref-Howe_2007_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howe_2007-7">[6]</a></sup> Historians have in the aggregate <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of presidents of the United States">ranked</a> Adams as the 21st most successful president.</p>
<p>After leaving office, he was elected as <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. Representative</a> from <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> in 1830, serving for the last 17 years of his life with greater acclaim than he had achieved as president. Animated by <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams_and_abolitionism" title="John Quincy Adams and abolitionism">his growing revulsion against slavery</a>, Adams became a leading opponent of the <a href="/wiki/Slave_Power" title="Slave Power">Slave Power</a>. Adams predicted the Union's dissolution over slavery, and in such a case, felt the president could abolish slavery by using his <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States#War_and_foreign_affairs_powers" title="President of the United States">war powers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Parsons_1998_page.3D162_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parsons_1998_page.3D162-8">[7]</a></sup> Adams also became a critic of the <a href="/wiki/Annexation_of_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="Annexation of Texas">annexation of Texas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mexican-American_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexican-American War">Mexican-American War</a>, which he saw as an aggressive war for territory.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life.2C_education.2C_and_early_career">Early life, education, and early career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life, education, and early career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>John Quincy Adams was born on July 11, 1767, to <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Abigail_Adams" title="Abigail Adams">Abigail</a> (née Smith) in a part of <a href="/wiki/Braintree,_Massachusetts" title="Braintree, Massachusetts">Braintree</a> <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect" title="Province of Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> that is now <a href="/wiki/Quincy,_Massachusetts" title="Quincy, Massachusetts">Quincy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> He was named for his mother's maternal grandfather, <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy" title="John Quincy">Colonel John Quincy</a>, after whom Quincy, Massachusetts, is named.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> Young Adams was educated by private tutors – his cousin James Thaxter and his father's law clerk, Nathan Rice.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> He soon began to exhibit his literary skills in 1779, when he initiated a diary which he kept until just before he died in 1848.<sup id="cite_ref-Masshist.org_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Masshist.org-12">[11]</a></sup> The diary comprised an unprecedented fifty volumes, representing one of the most extensive and widely cited collections of first-hand information about the early republic.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Much of Adams' youth was spent accompanying his father overseas. He accompanied his father on diplomatic missions to <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_France" title="United States Ambassador to France">France</a> from 1778 until 1779 and to the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> from 1780 until 1782.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> Adams acquired an education at institutions such as <a href="/wiki/Leiden_University" title="Leiden University">Leiden University</a>. He matriculated in Leiden on January 10, 1781.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> For nearly three years, beginning at the age of 14, he accompanied <a href="/wiki/Francis_Dana" title="Francis Dana">Francis Dana</a> as a secretary on a mission to <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, to obtain recognition of the new United States. He spent time in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> and, in 1804, published a travel report on <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> During these years overseas, Adams <a href="/wiki/List_of_multilingual_presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of multilingual presidents of the United States">became fluent in</a> French and Dutch and became familiar with <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> and other European languages.</p>
<p>Though Adams enjoyed Europe, he and his family decided he needed to return to the United States to complete his education and eventually launch a political career.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> He entered <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College" title="Harvard College">Harvard College</a>, graduated in 1787 with a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Arts" title="Bachelor of Arts">Bachelor of Arts</a> degree, and was elected by <a href="/wiki/Phi_Beta_Kappa" class="mw-redirect" title="Phi Beta Kappa">Phi Beta Kappa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> Adams, mainly with the influence of his father, had excelled in classical studies and reached fluency in Latin and Greek. Upon entering Harvard he had already translated Virgil, Horace, Plutarch, and Aristotle<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> and within six months memorized his Greek grammar and translated the New Testament.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> After graduating from Harvard, he <a href="/wiki/Reading_law" title="Reading law">studied law</a> with <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Parsons" title="Theophilus Parsons">Theophilus Parsons</a> in <a href="/wiki/Newburyport,_Massachusetts" title="Newburyport, Massachusetts">Newburyport, Massachusetts</a> from 1787 to 1789.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> He earned a <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Arts" title="Master of Arts">Master of Arts</a> from Harvard in 1790, <a href="/wiki/Admission_to_practice_law" title="Admission to practice law">was admitted to the bar</a> in 1791, and began <a href="/wiki/Practice_of_law" title="Practice of law">practicing law</a> in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career_.281796.E2.80.931817.29">Early political career (1796–1817)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early political career (1796–1817)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Washington_and_Adams_administrations">Washington and Adams administrations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Washington and Adams administrations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams,_by_John_Singleton_Copley.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/John_Quincy_Adams%2C_by_John_Singleton_Copley.jpg/220px-John_Quincy_Adams%2C_by_John_Singleton_Copley.jpg" width="220" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/John_Quincy_Adams%2C_by_John_Singleton_Copley.jpg/330px-John_Quincy_Adams%2C_by_John_Singleton_Copley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/John_Quincy_Adams%2C_by_John_Singleton_Copley.jpg/440px-John_Quincy_Adams%2C_by_John_Singleton_Copley.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1285" data-file-height="1516" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams,_by_John_Singleton_Copley.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Quincy Adams, age 29</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adams initially won national recognition when he published a series of articles supporting Washington's decision to keep America out of the growing hostilities surrounding the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> Soon after, <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> appointed Adams, age 26, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_the_Netherlands" title="United States Ambassador to the Netherlands">minister to the Netherlands</a> in 1793. He preferred his quiet life of reading in Massachusetts, but yielded to his father's persuasion to take it. On his way to the Netherlands, he delivered a set of documents to <a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">John Jay</a>, who was negotiating the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a>; after spending some time with Jay, Adams wrote home to his father, in support of the emerging treaty, because he thought America should stay out of European affairs. Historian <a href="/wiki/Paul_C._Nagel" title="Paul C. Nagel">Paul Nagel</a> has noted that this letter ultimately reached Washington, and that parts of it were used by Washington when drafting his <a href="/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Farewell_Address" title="George Washington's Farewell Address">farewell address</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>While going back and forth between The Hague and London, he met and proposed to his future wife, <a href="/wiki/Louisa_Adams" title="Louisa Adams">Louisa Catherine Johnson</a>. Though he wanted to return to private life at the end of his appointment, Washington named him <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Portugal" title="United States Ambassador to Portugal">minister to Portugal</a> in 1796, where he was soon assigned to the Berlin Legation. Though his talents were far greater than his desire to serve, he was finally convinced to remain in public service when he learned how highly Washington regarded his abilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart,_1821-26.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart%2C_1821-26.jpg/170px-Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart%2C_1821-26.jpg" width="170" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart%2C_1821-26.jpg/255px-Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart%2C_1821-26.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart%2C_1821-26.jpg/340px-Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart%2C_1821-26.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2517" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Louisa_Catherine_Johnson_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart,_1821-26.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Stuart" title="Gilbert Stuart">Gilbert Stuart</a> <i>Portrait of Louisa Adams (1821-26)</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Washington called Adams "the most valuable of America's officials abroad," and Nagel believes that it was at this time that Adams came to terms with a future of public service.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> While abroad, Adams continued to urge neutrality, arguing that the United States would benefit economically by staying out of the ongoing <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">French Revolutionary Wars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>He became a Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a> in 1797.<sup id="cite_ref-AAAS_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS-21">[20]</a></sup> When the elder Adams became president, he appointed his son in 1797 as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Germany" title="United States Ambassador to Germany">Minister to Prussia</a> at Washington's urging.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> There Adams signed the renewal of the very liberal Prussian-American Treaty of Amity and Commerce after negotiations with Prussian Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Count_Karl-Wilhelm_Finck_von_Finckenstein" title="Count Karl-Wilhelm Finck von Finckenstein">Count Karl-Wilhelm Finck von Finckenstein</a>. He served at that post until 1801, when <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> took office as president.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>While serving abroad, in 1797 Adams also married Louisa Catherine Johnson, the daughter of a poor American merchant, in a ceremony at the church of <a href="/wiki/All_Hallows-by-the-Tower" title="All Hallows-by-the-Tower">All Hallows-by-the-Tower</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>. Adams is the first president to marry a first lady born outside of the United States, and this did not recur until President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> assumed office in 2017, with <a href="/wiki/Melania_Trump" title="Melania Trump">Melania Trump</a> as first lady.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Massachusetts_politics">Massachusetts politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Massachusetts politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On his return to the United States, Adams was appointed a Commissioner of Monetary Affairs in Boston by a Federal District Judge, but this was rescinded by Thomas Jefferson. He again tried his hand as an attorney, but soon entered politics; he was elected a member of the Massachusetts State Senate in April 1802. In November of that same year he ran unsuccessfully as a Federalist for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Massachusetts state legislature, then referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_General_Court" title="Massachusetts General Court">Massachusetts General Court</a>, in 1803 elected Adams as a Federalist to the U.S. Senate, where he served until 1808, at which time he broke with the Federalist Party. Adams as a senator had supported the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a> and Jefferson's <a href="/wiki/Embargo_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Embargo Act">Embargo Act</a>, which made him very unpopular with that party. The Federalist-controlled Massachusetts Legislature chose a replacement for Adams on June 3, 1808, several months early. On June 8, Adams renounced his membership in the party, resigned his Senate seat, and became a Republican.<sup id="cite_ref-NPS_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPS-26">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harvard_professor">Harvard professor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Harvard professor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While a member of the Senate, Adams also served as a professor of logic at <a href="/wiki/Brown_University" title="Brown University">Brown University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> Disowned by the Federalists and not fully accepted by the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Republicans</a>, he then accepted the Boylston Professorship of Rhetoric and Oratory at Harvard, and used this as a platform; he assumed this position in 1805 after declining the presidency of <a href="/wiki/Harvard" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvard">Harvard</a>. Adams' devotion to classical rhetoric shaped his response to public issues. He remained inspired by those rhetorical ideals long after the neo-classicalism and deferential politics of the founding generation were eclipsed by the commercial ethos and mass democracy of the Jacksonian Era. Many of Adams' idiosyncratic positions were rooted in his abiding devotion to the Ciceronian ideal of the citizen-orator "speaking well" to promote the welfare of the polis.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> He was also influenced by the classical republican ideal of civic eloquence espoused by British philosopher <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> Adams adapted these classical republican ideals of public oratory to the American debate, viewing its multilevel political structure as ripe for "the renaissance of Demosthenic eloquence." His <i>Lectures on Rhetoric and Oratory</i> (1810) looks at the fate of ancient oratory, the necessity of liberty for it to flourish, and its importance as a unifying element for a new nation of diverse cultures and beliefs. Just as civic eloquence failed to gain popularity in Britain, in the United States interest faded in the second decade of the 19th century as the "public spheres of heated oratory" disappeared in favor of the private sphere.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_U.S._minister_to_Russia">First U.S. minister to Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: First U.S. minister to Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>President <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> appointed Adams as the first <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Russia" title="United States Ambassador to Russia">United States Minister to Russia</a> in 1809. Though Adams had only recently broken with the Federalist Party, his support of Jefferson's foreign policy had earned him goodwill with the Madison Administration.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> After resigning his post at Harvard, Adams and his wife Louisa boarded a merchant ship in Boston on Aug. 5, 1809. Their youngest son was with them during the long and tedious voyage to St. Petersburg which was temporarily interrupted outside the southern coast of Norway<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> due to the <a href="/wiki/Gunboat_War" title="Gunboat War">Gunboat War</a>. They were at first boarded by a British officer who examined their papers and then, later that day, by a <a href="/wiki/Norwegians" title="Norwegians">Norwegian</a> officer who ordered the ship to <a href="/wiki/Kristiansand" title="Kristiansand">Christiansand</a>. In Christiansand, Adams discovered thirty-eight U.S. vessels had been detained by the <a href="/wiki/Norwegians" title="Norwegians">Norwegians</a> and determined to gain the release of both ships and crew as soon as possible. The voyage to St. Petersburg resumed but was once again stopped by a British squadron. Adams showed his commission to <a href="/wiki/Sir_Albemarle_Bertie,_1st_Baronet" title="Sir Albemarle Bertie, 1st Baronet">Admiral Albermarle Bertie</a>, the commander of the Squadron who recognized Adams as an ambassador. Because of the many delays, the Adamses did not arrive in St. Petersburg until October 23, 1809.<sup id="cite_ref-Josiah_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Josiah-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Count <a href="/wiki/Nikolay_Rumyantsev" title="Nikolay Rumyantsev">Nikolay Rumyantsev</a>, Chancellor of the empire, formally received Adams, and requested a copy of his credential letter. Romanzoff assured Adams that his appointment pleased him personally. Adams' presentation to the emperor was postponed, however, because of the temporary indisposition of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Russia" title="Alexander I of Russia">Alexander I</a>. Rumyantsev immediately invited Adams to a diplomatic dinner which included the French ambassador, <a href="/wiki/Armand_Augustin_Louis_de_Caulaincourt" class="mw-redirect" title="Armand Augustin Louis de Caulaincourt">Armand Augustin Louis de Caulaincourt</a>, Duke of Vicenza, numerous foreign ministers then at the Russian Court, and many of the nobility. This was the same mansion where Adams had dined in 1781, as secretary of Francis Dana.<sup id="cite_ref-Josiah_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Josiah-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tsar Alexander I received Adams alone in his cabinet where he expressed his pleasure at Adams' appointment. Adams told Alexander that "the president of the United States had desired him to express the hope that his mission would be considered as a proof of respect for the person and character of his majesty, as an acknowledgment of the many testimonies of good-will he had already given to the United States, and of a desire to strengthen commercial relations between them and his provinces." Alexander replied, that, "in everything depending on him, he should be happy to contribute to the increase of their friendly relations; that it was his wish to establish a just system of maritime rights, and that he should adhere invariably to those he had declared." After these official diplomatic greetings, Alexander and Adams discussed several other issues such as the policies of the different European powers, trade and commerce, and other mutually beneficial prospects, and that Russia and U.S. could be very useful to each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Josiah_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Josiah-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adams urged Rumyantsev to ask Alexander to act on behalf of the United States in securing the release of the American sailors and ships being held by the Danish. The Tsar ordered the Chancellor to request the release of the American property as soon as possible, which the Danish government complied with. Adams spent a great deal of time securing the release of American vessels and seamen from various "seizures and sequestrations."<sup id="cite_ref-Josiah_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Josiah-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1811, Adams received a commission from the Secretary of State to serve as an Associate Justice of the Supreme Court of the United States; he promptly declined and remained in St. Petersburg. In 1812, Adams witnessed and reported the news of <a href="/wiki/French_invasion_of_Russia" title="French invasion of Russia">Napoleon's invasion of Russia</a> and the latter's disastrous retreat. Also in 1812, Rumyantsev asked if he should request Alexander to mediate a pacification of hostilities between the United States and Great Britain. The U.S. accepted the offer and in July 1813, two associates of Adams, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Gallatin" title="Albert Gallatin">Albert Gallatin</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_A._Bayard_(elder)" title="James A. Bayard (elder)">James A. Bayard</a> arrived in St. Petersburg to begin negotiations under mediation by Alexander. Gallatin was at that time Secretary of Treasury and the Senate rejected his appointment to the diplomatic mission as unconstitutional. However, this rejection did not occur until after Gallatin and Bayard had left for St. Petersburg. In September, <a href="/wiki/Earl_Cathcart" title="Earl Cathcart">Lord William Cathcart</a> delivered a British message to Alexander explaining their reasons for declining the mediation. Thus ended President Madison's hope that Alexander could end the war.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg/220px-1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg" width="220" height="319" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg/330px-1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg/440px-1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3201" data-file-height="4647" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1815_US_passport_-_LONDON.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1815 US passport issued by John Quincy Adams at London</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Louisa Adams served an invaluable role as wife-of-diplomat, becoming a favorite of the czar and making up for her husband's utter lack of charm.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> However, Adams was personally well liked by the Russian Court and often would be met on walks by Alexander. The Tsar asked Adams if he would be taking a house in the country over the summer. When Adams hesitated, the emperor stated with good humor that perhaps it was a financial consideration and Adams was able to respond in kind that it indeed was in large part. Adams was a man who endeavored to live within the means provided by the American government.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minister_to_the_Court_of_St._James.27s">Minister to the Court of St. James's</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Minister to the Court of St. James's">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1814, Adams was recalled from Russia to serve as chief negotiator of the U.S. commission for the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ghent" title="Treaty of Ghent">Treaty of Ghent</a>, which ended the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> between the United States and United Kingdom. The United Kingdom's first peace offer in mid-1814 was unacceptable to the American delegation, as it included unfavorable terms such as the creation of an <a href="/wiki/Indian_barrier_state" title="Indian barrier state">Indian barrier state</a> out of parts of the northwestern United States. By November 1814, the government of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Jenkinson,_2nd_Earl_of_Liverpool" title="Robert Jenkinson, 2nd Earl of Liverpool">Lord Liverpool</a> decided to seek an end to hostilities with the U.S. on the basis of <i>status quo ante bellum</i>. Adams and his fellow commissioners had hoped for similar terms, though a return to the status quo would mean the continuation of British practice of <a href="/wiki/Impressment" title="Impressment">impressment</a>, which had been a major cause of the war. The treaty was signed on December 24, 1814. The United States did not gain any concessions from the treaty, but could boast that it had survived a war against the strongest power in the world. Following the signing of the treaty, Adams traveled to Paris, where he witnessed first-hand the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days" title="Hundred Days">Hundred Days</a> of Napoleon's restoration. During this period, Adams learned that President Madison had appointed him as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="United States Ambassador to the United Kingdom">minister to the Court of St. James's</a> (<a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adams arrived in the Britain in May 1815.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> In London, Adams was part of a U.S. Legation consisting of himself, two young secretaries and a small office in Craven Street, near <a href="/wiki/Charing_Cross" title="Charing Cross">Charing Cross</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sisters_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sisters-39">[38]</a></sup> Since they were not particularly well paid, Adams and his wife Louisa lived in <a href="/wiki/Ealing" title="Ealing">Ealing</a>, at that time a village in the countryside, in order to maintain the expensive carriages and liveries which social appearance demanded.<sup id="cite_ref-sisters_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sisters-39">[38]</a></sup> With the aid of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Gallatin" title="Albert Gallatin">Albert Gallatin</a>, who had also been part of the U.S. commission at Ghent, Adams negotiated a limited trade agreement with Britain. Following the conclusion of the trade agreement, much of Adams's time as ambassador was spent helping stranded American sailors and prisoners of war. In April 1817, Adams received a letter from newly-inaugurated President <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a>, who offered Adams the role of Secretary of State. Having spent several years in Europe, Adams finally returned to the United States in August 1817.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Secretary_of_State_.281817.E2.80.931825.29">U.S. Secretary of State (1817–1825)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: U.S. Secretary of State (1817–1825)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/225px-Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="225" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/338px-Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/450px-Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3883" data-file-height="4697" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_Stuart_-_John_Quincy_Adams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Adams portrait - <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Stuart" title="Gilbert Stuart">Gilbert Stuart</a>, 1818</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In pursuit of national unity, President Monroe decided a Northerner would be optimal for the position of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>, and he chose the respected and experienced Adams for the role.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> Adams served as Secretary of State throughout Monroe's eight-year presidency, from 1817 to 1825. Taking office in the aftermath of the War of 1812, Adams thought that the country had been fortunate in avoiding territorial losses, and he prioritized avoiding another war with a European power, particularly Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Kaplan_2014_pages.3D321.E2.80.93322_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan_2014_pages.3D321.E2.80.93322-42">[41]</a></sup> One of the major challenges confronting Adams was how to respond to the power vacuum in Latin America arising from Spain's weakness following the <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_War" title="Peninsular War">Peninsular War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> Monroe and Adams agreed on most of the major foreign policy issues: both favored neutrality towards the <a href="/wiki/Latin_American_wars_of_independence" title="Latin American wars of independence">Latin American wars of independence</a>, peace with Great Britain, denial of a trade agreement with the French, and expansion, peacefully if possible, into the North American territories of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adams had begun negotiations with Britain during his time as ambassador over several contentious issues that had not been solved by the War of 1812 or the Treaty of Ghent. In 1817, the two countries agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Rush%E2%80%93Bagot_Treaty" title="Rush–Bagot Treaty">Rush–Bagot Treaty</a>, which limited naval armaments on the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a>. Negotiations between the two powers continued, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_1818" title="Treaty of 1818">Treaty of 1818</a>, which defined the <a href="/wiki/Canada%E2%80%93United_States_border" title="Canada–United States border">Canada–United States border</a> west of the Great Lakes. The boundary was set at the <a href="/wiki/49th_parallel_north" title="49th parallel north">49th parallel</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a>, while the territory to the west of the mountains (<a href="/wiki/Oregon_Country" title="Oregon Country">Oregon Country</a>) would be jointly occupied. The agreement represented a turning point in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom%E2%80%93United_States_relations" title="United Kingdom–United States relations">United Kingdom–United States relations</a>, as the U.S. turned its attention to its southern and western borders and British fears over American expansionism waned.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Adams_onis_map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Adams_onis_map.png/350px-Adams_onis_map.png" width="350" height="286" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Adams_onis_map.png/525px-Adams_onis_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Adams_onis_map.png/700px-Adams_onis_map.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="654" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Adams_onis_map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In the <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">Adams–Onís Treaty</a>, the United States acquired Florida and set the western border of the 1803 <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Adams took office, Spanish possessions bordered the United States to the South and West. In the South, Spain retained control of <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Florida" title="Spanish Florida">Florida</a>, which the U.S. had long sought to purchase. Spain struggled to control the <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> tribes active in Florida, some of which raided U.S. territory. In the West, <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">New Spain</a> bordered the territory purchased by the U.S. in the Louisiana Purchase, but no clear boundary had been established between U.S. and Spanish territory.<sup id="cite_ref-Kaplan_2014_pages.3D321.E2.80.93322_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan_2014_pages.3D321.E2.80.93322-42">[41]</a></sup> After taking office, Adams began negotiations with <a href="/wiki/Luis_de_On%C3%ADs" title="Luis de Onís">Luis de Onís</a>, the Spanish minister to the United States, for the purchase of Florida and the settlement of a border between the U.S. and New Spain. Adams hoped to set the U.S. border at the <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Rio Grande River">Rio Grande River</a>, while the Spanish preferred that the border be set at the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a>. The negotiations were interrupted by an escalation of the <a href="/wiki/Seminole_Wars" title="Seminole Wars">Seminole War</a>, and in December 1818 Monroe ordered General <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> to enter Florida and retaliate against <a href="/wiki/Seminole" title="Seminole">Seminoles</a> that had raided Georgia. Exceeding his orders, Jackson defeated the Seminoles, but also captured the Spanish outposts of St. Marks and <a href="/wiki/Pensacola,_Florida" title="Pensacola, Florida">Pensacola</a> and executed two Englishmen. While the rest of the cabinet was outraged by Jackson's actions, Adams defended them as necessary to self-defense and protected by international law, and Adams eventually convinced Monroe and most of the cabinet to support Jackson. After extended negotiations, the two powers agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">Adams–Onís Treaty</a>, in which Spain transferred Florida to the U.S. and the <a href="/wiki/Sabine_River_(Texas%E2%80%93Louisiana)" title="Sabine River (Texas–Louisiana)">Sabine River</a> was established as the western border of the United States. The treaty was ratified in February 1821.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the Spanish Empire continued to fracture during Monroe's second term, Adams and Monroe became increasingly concerned that the "<a href="/wiki/Holy_Alliance" title="Holy Alliance">Holy Alliance</a>" (which consisted of <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>) would seek to bring Spain's erstwhile colonies under control. In 1822, following the conclusion of the Adams–Onís Treaty, the Monroe administration recognized the independence of several Latin American countries, including Argentina and Mexico. In 1823, British Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/George_Canning" title="George Canning">George Canning</a> suggested that the U.S. and Britain should work together to preserve the independence of these fledgling republics. The cabinet debated whether or not to accept the offer, but Adams opposed it. Instead, Adams urged Monroe to publicly declare U.S. opposition to any European attempt to colonize or re-take control of territory in the Americas, while also committing the U.S. to neutrality with respect to European affairs. Adams wrote a draft for Monroe that stated these principles and also proclaimed U.S. support for republican principles. In his December 1823 <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union" title="State of the Union">annual message to Congress</a>, Monroe laid out the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a>, which was largely built upon Adams's ideas. The doctrine became one of the foundational principles of <a href="/wiki/History_of_U.S._foreign_policy" title="History of U.S. foreign policy">U.S. foreign policy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1824_presidential_election">1824 presidential election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1824 presidential election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1824.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/ElectoralCollege1824.svg/250px-ElectoralCollege1824.svg.png" width="250" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/ElectoralCollege1824.svg/375px-ElectoralCollege1824.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/ElectoralCollege1824.svg/500px-ElectoralCollege1824.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="577" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1824.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1824 presidential election results</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Immediately upon becoming Secretary of State, Adams emerged as one of Monroe's most likely successors, as the last three presidents had all served in the role (although Jefferson also served as vice president) before taking office. As the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1824" title="United States presidential election, 1824">1824 election</a> approached, Adams, Clay, Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>, and Secretary of the Treasury <a href="/wiki/William_H._Crawford" title="William H. Crawford">William H. Crawford</a> positioned themselves to succeed Monroe. Adams felt that his own election as president would vindicate his father, while also allowing him to pursue an ambitious domestic policy. Though he lacked the charisma of his competitors, Adams was widely respected and benefited from the lack of other prominent Northerners.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Federalist Party had nearly collapsed in the aftermath of the War of 1812, and all of the major presidential candidates were members of Monroe's Democratic-Republican Party. As 1824 approached, Jackson jumped into the race, motivated in large part by his anger over Clay and Crawford's denunciations of his actions in Florida.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Congressional_nominating_caucus" title="Congressional nominating caucus">congressional nominating caucus</a> had decided upon previous presidential nominees, but it had become largely discredited. Candidates were instead nominated by state legislatures or nominating conventions, and Adams received the endorsement of several New England legislatures.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> Seeing Jackson's strength, Calhoun dropped out of the presidential race and instead sought the vice presidency. The remaining candidates relied heavily on regional strength. Adams was popular in New England, Clay and Jackson were strong in the West, and Jackson and Crawford competed for the South, despite the latter's health problems. In the 1824 presidential election, no candidate won a majority of the electoral vote, necessitating a contingent election under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twelfth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twelfth Amendment</a>. The House decided among the top three electoral vote winners, with each state's delegation having one vote. The top three electoral vote winners were Jackson, Adams, and Crawford; though Clay had also received electoral votes, he was not eligible to be selected by the House.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Election_of_1825.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/House_Election_of_1825.svg/400px-House_Election_of_1825.svg.png" width="400" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/House_Election_of_1825.svg/600px-House_Election_of_1825.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/House_Election_of_1825.svg/800px-House_Election_of_1825.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1020" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Election_of_1825.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Voting by state in the 1824 contingent election. States in <span style="color:#ff9955"><b>orange</b></span> voted for Adams, states in <span style="color:#5fd35f"><b>green</b></span> for Crawford, and states in <span style="color:#698dc5"><b>blue</b></span> for Jackson.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adams knew that his own victory in the contingent election would require the support of Clay, who besides being a presidential contender also had accumulated immense influence in the House and had thrice served as the body's speaker. In contrast with Clay, Crawford believed in a weak, limited federal government. Jackson's policy views were unclear, but Clay had been outraged by Jackson's actions in Florida, and he feared what Jackson would do in office. Clay's <a href="/wiki/American_System_(economic_plan)" title="American System (economic plan)">American System</a> called for high <a href="/wiki/Tariffs_in_United_States_history" title="Tariffs in United States history">tariffs</a>, federally-funded <a href="/wiki/Public_works" title="Public works">internal improvements</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/History_of_central_banking_in_the_United_States" title="History of central banking in the United States">national bank</a>, all of which were supported by Adams. Adams and Clay met prior to the contingent election, and Clay agreed to support Adams. In February 1825 Adams won the contingent election, taking thirteen of the twenty-four state delegations. After the election, many of Jackson's supporters claimed that Adams and Clay had reached a "<a href="/wiki/Corrupt_Bargain" title="Corrupt Bargain">Corrupt Bargain</a>" in which Adams promised Clay the position of Secretary of State in return for Clay's support.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281825.E2.80.931829.29">Presidency (1825–1829)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Presidency (1825–1829)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_John_Quincy_Adams" title="Presidency of John Quincy Adams">Presidency of John Quincy Adams</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_by_GPA_Healy,_1858.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/John_Quincy_Adams_by_GPA_Healy%2C_1858.jpg/200px-John_Quincy_Adams_by_GPA_Healy%2C_1858.jpg" width="200" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/John_Quincy_Adams_by_GPA_Healy%2C_1858.jpg/300px-John_Quincy_Adams_by_GPA_Healy%2C_1858.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/John_Quincy_Adams_by_GPA_Healy%2C_1858.jpg/400px-John_Quincy_Adams_by_GPA_Healy%2C_1858.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2284" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_by_GPA_Healy,_1858.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Quincy Adams, <a href="/wiki/George_Peter_Alexander_Healy" title="George Peter Alexander Healy">George Peter Alexander Healy</a>, 1858</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inauguration">Inauguration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Inauguration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Adams was <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_John_Quincy_Adams" title="Inauguration of John Quincy Adams">inaugurated</a> on March 4, 1825. He took the <a href="/wiki/Oath_of_office_of_the_president_of_the_United_States#Use_of_Bibles" class="mw-redirect" title="Oath of office of the president of the United States">oath of office</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_law" title="Constitutional law">book of constitutional law</a>, instead of the more traditional <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> In his inaugural address, adopted a post-partisan tone, promising that he would avoid party-building and politically-motivated appointments. He also proposed an elaborate program of "internal improvements": roads, ports, and canals. Though some worried about the constitutionality of such federal projects, Adams argued that the <a href="/wiki/Taxing_and_Spending_Clause" title="Taxing and Spending Clause">General Welfare Clause</a> provided for broad constitutional authority. While his predecessors had engaged in projects like the building of the <a href="/wiki/National_Road" title="National Road">National Road</a>, Adams promised that he would ask Congress to authorize many more such projects.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Appointments">Appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_cabinet">Administration and cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Administration and cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
