<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Judiciary">Judiciary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Judiciary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Adams <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_John_Quincy_Adams" title="List of federal judges appointed by John Quincy Adams">appointed</a> one Justice to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> and eleven judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_courts" class="mw-redirect" title="United States district courts">United States district courts</a>. The lone Supreme Court Justice, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Trimble" title="Robert Trimble">Robert Trimble</a>, served from May 1826 to his death in August 1828. Adams nominated <a href="/wiki/John_J._Crittenden" title="John J. Crittenden">John J. Crittenden</a> to replace Trimble, but the Senate never voted on Crittenden's nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_policies">Domestic policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Domestic policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In his 1825 annual message to Congress, Adams presented a comprehensive and ambitious agenda. He called for major investments in internal improvements as well as the creation of a national university, a naval academy, and a national astronomical observatory. Noting the healthy status of the treasury and the possibility for more revenue via land sales, Adams argued for the completion of several projects that were in various stages of construction or planning, including a road from Washington to <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> However, Adams's programs faced opposition from various quarters. Many disagreed with his broad interpretation of the constitution, and favored stronger state governments at the expense of the federal government. Others disliked any government interference and were opposed to central planning.<sup id="cite_ref-Kaplan_2014_pages.3D404.E2.80.93405_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan_2014_pages.3D404.E2.80.93405-57">[56]</a></sup> Some in the South feared that Adams was secretly an <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">abolitionist</a> and that he sought to suborn the states to the federal government.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of his proposals were adopted, specifically the extension of the <a href="/wiki/Cumberland_Road" class="mw-redirect" title="Cumberland Road">Cumberland Road</a> into <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> with surveys for its continuation west to <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis</a>; the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_and_Ohio_Canal" title="Chesapeake and Ohio Canal">Chesapeake and Ohio Canal</a>, the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_and_Delaware_Canal" class="mw-redirect" title="Chesapeake and Delaware Canal">Chesapeake and Delaware Canal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Louisville_and_Portland_Canal" title="Louisville and Portland Canal">Louisville and Portland Canal</a> around the <a href="/wiki/Falls_of_the_Ohio" class="mw-redirect" title="Falls of the Ohio">falls of the Ohio</a>; the connection of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River system</a> in Ohio and Indiana; and the enlargement and rebuilding of the <a href="/wiki/Dismal_Swamp_Canal" title="Dismal Swamp Canal">Dismal Swamp Canal</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> One of the issues which divided the administration was protective tariffs, of which Henry Clay was a leading advocate.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Allies of Adams lost control of Congress after the 1826 mid-term elections, and pro-Adams Speaker of the House John Taylor was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Stevenson" title="Andrew Stevenson">Andrew Stevenson</a>, a Jackson supporter.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> Jacksonian Congressmen lobbied numerous attacks against Adams, including attacks on Adams's actions at Ghent and criticism of White House expenditures such as the purchase of a pool table.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> Jacksonians devised the <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1828" class="mw-redirect" title="Tariff of 1828">Tariff of 1828</a>, which raised tariffs considerably. After signing the tariff, Adams was denounced in the South, but received little credit for the tariff in the North.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adams sought the gradual assimilation of Native Americans via consensual agreements, a priority shared by few whites in the 1820s. Yet Adams was also deeply committed to the westward expansion of the United States. Settlers on the frontier, who were constantly seeking to move westward, cried for a more expansionist policy that disregarded the concerns of a supposedly inferior civilization. Early in his term, Adams suspended the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Indian_Springs_(1825)" title="Treaty of Indian Springs (1825)">Treaty of Indian Springs</a> after learning that the Governor of Georgia, <a href="/wiki/George_Troup" title="George Troup">George Troup</a>, had forced the treaty on the <a href="/wiki/Muscogee" title="Muscogee">Muscogee</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> Adams signed a new treaty with the Muskogee in January 1826 that allowed the Muskogee to stay but ceded most of their land to Georgia. Troup refused to accept its terms, and authorized all Georgian citizens to evict the Muskogee. A third treaty was signed in 1828, giving all of the Muskogee land to Georgia.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policies">Foreign policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Foreign policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="width: 230px; height: 270px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -55px; left: -45px; width: 325px"><a href="/wiki/File:ADAMS,_John_Q-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Adams as president" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/ADAMS%2C_John_Q-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/325px-ADAMS%2C_John_Q-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="325" height="380" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/ADAMS%2C_John_Q-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/488px-ADAMS%2C_John_Q-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/ADAMS%2C_John_Q-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/650px-ADAMS%2C_John_Q-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6902" data-file-height="8066" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ADAMS,_John_Q-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:ADAMS, John Q-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:ADAMS, John Q-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Adams as president</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to Charles Edel, Adams believed that, "Intervention would accomplish little, retard the cause of republicanism, and distract the country from its primary goal of continental expansion." Moreover, fearful that U.S. intentions would outstrip its capabilities, Adams thought that projecting U.S. power abroad would weaken its gravitational force on the North American continent.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>During his term as president, Adams achieved little of long-term consequence in foreign affairs. A reason for this was the opposition he faced in Congress, where his rivals prevented him from succeeding.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> Among his diplomatic achievements were treaties of <a href="/wiki/Reciprocity_(international_relations)" title="Reciprocity (international relations)">reciprocity</a> with a number of nations, including <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a> countries, <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>. However, thanks to the successes of Adams' diplomacy during his previous eight years as secretary of state, most of the foreign policy issues he would have faced had been resolved by the time he became president.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>As president, Adams continued to pursue the peaceful settlement of potential disputes with Britain, including the unsettled border between Maine and Canada. However, in 1825, Britain banned U.S. trade from the British West Indies, damaging Adams's prestige in foreign affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> After Congress retaliated by increasing tariffs on British products, the British forbid U.S. trade with any British colony aside from Canada, further damaging U.S. businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adams favored sending a U.S. delegation to the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Panama" title="Congress of Panama">Congress of Panama</a>, an 1826 conference of New World republics organized by <a href="/wiki/Sim%C3%B3n_Bol%C3%ADvar" title="Simón Bolívar">Simón Bolívar</a>. Adams sought closer ties with the new Latin American states, believing that stability among the new states would benefit the U.S. and be conducive for the purchase of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> from <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> However, the funding for a delegation and the confirmation of delegation nominees became entangled in a political battle over Adams's domestic policies, with opponents such as Senator <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a> impeding the process of confirming a delegation.<sup id="cite_ref-Kaplan_2014_pages.3D404.E2.80.93405_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan_2014_pages.3D404.E2.80.93405-57">[56]</a></sup> Though the delegation finally won confirmation from the Senate, it never reached the Congress of Panama due to congressional resistance and delay.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1828_presidential_election">1828 presidential election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: 1828 presidential election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1828" title="United States presidential election, 1828">United States presidential election, 1828</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1828.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/ElectoralCollege1828.svg/250px-ElectoralCollege1828.svg.png" width="250" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/ElectoralCollege1828.svg/375px-ElectoralCollege1828.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/ElectoralCollege1828.svg/500px-ElectoralCollege1828.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="577" data-file-height="593" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1828.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1828 presidential election results</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During his presidency, Adams's opponents coalesced around Jackson. Opponents accused Adams of favoring big government, the Northeast, manufacturing, and abolition. Followers of Jackson, Van Buren, and Calhoun formed a proto-party apparatus, raising large sums of money and sponsoring newspapers and local clubs.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> Adams, meanwhile, refused to adapt to the new reality of political campaigns, and he avoided public functions and refused to invest in pro-administration tools such as newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1828" title="United States presidential election, 1828">presidential election of 1828</a> approached, Jackson was viewed as the favorite by many, as Van Buren and others had established a strong base of support. In the spring of 1827, Jackson was publicly accused of having encouraged his wife to desert her first husband.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> In response, followers of Jackson attacked Adams's personal life, and the campaign turned increasingly nasty.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> Though few doubted Adams's intelligence, the Jacksonian press portrayed him as an out-of-touch elitist.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Vice President Calhoun joined Jackson's ticket, while Adams turned to Secretary of the Treasury <a href="/wiki/Richard_Rush" title="Richard Rush">Richard Rush</a> as his running mate. This represented the first time in U.S. history that a ticket of two Northerners faced at ticket of two Southerners.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> Neither side publicly campaigned on the issue of slavery, but Adams's status as a New Englander may have hurt him, as many outside of New England held negative cultural stereotypes about the region.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The key states in the election were New York, Pennsylvania, and Ohio, which accounted for nearly a third of the country's electoral votes.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> Jackson won Pennsylvania, Ohio, and even Clay's home state of Kentucky. He also won a majority of the electoral votes in New York, and denied Adams a sweep of New England by winning an electoral vote in Maine. In the South, aside from Adams's win in Maryland, only Louisiana was remotely competitive, and even there Jackson won 53% of the vote. In total, Jackson won 178 of the 261 electoral votes and just under 56 percent of the popular vote. No future presidential candidate would match Jackson's proportion of the popular vote until <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> exceeded it in 1904. Adams's loss made him the second one-term president, after his own father. The election marked the permanent end of the Era of Good Feelings and the start of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Party_System" title="Second Party System">Second Party System</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Departure">Departure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Departure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>John Quincy Adams left office on March 4, 1829. Adams did not attend the inauguration of his successor, Andrew Jackson, who had openly snubbed him by refusing to pay the traditional "courtesy call" to the outgoing president during the weeks before his own inauguration.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> Jackson's wife had died shortly after the election, and Jackson blamed Adams and his followers for her death.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> Adams was one of only four presidents who chose not to attend their respective successor's inauguration; the others were <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">his father</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_congressional_career_.281830.E2.80.931848.29">Later congressional career (1830–1848)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Later congressional career (1830–1848)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Adams considered permanently retiring from public life after his 1828 defeat, and he was deeply hurt by the suicide of his son, <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Adams" title="George Washington Adams">George Washington Adams</a>, in 1829.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> He was appalled by many of the Jackson administration's actions, including its embrace of the <a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">spoils system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> Adams grew bored of his retirement and still felt that his career was unfinished, so he ran for and won a seat in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1830" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1830">1830 elections</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> His election went against the generally held opinion, shared by his own wife and youngest son, that former presidents should not run for public office.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> He was the first president to serve in <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> after his term of office, and one of only two former presidents to do so (<a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a> later served in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a>). He was elected to nine terms, serving as a Representative for 17 years, from 1831 until his death.<sup id="cite_ref-bioguide1_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bioguide1-86">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Returning to Washington at the age of sixty-four, Adams expected a light workload, but Speaker <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Stevenson" title="Andrew Stevenson">Andrew Stevenson</a> selected Adams chairman of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Energy_and_Commerce" title="United States House Committee on Energy and Commerce">Committee on Commerce and Manufactures</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> During his time in Congress he also served as chairman of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Natural_Resources" title="United States House Committee on Natural Resources">Committee on Indian Affairs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Foreign_Affairs" title="United States House Committee on Foreign Affairs">Committee on Foreign Affairs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bioguide1_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bioguide1-86">[85]</a></sup> Stevenson, an ally of Jackson, expected that the chairmanship would keep Adams busy defending the tariff, but that the presence of a Jacksonian majority on the committee would prevent Adams from accruing any real power.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adams ran for Governor of Massachusetts in 1833 on the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Masonic_Party" title="Anti-Masonic Party">Anti-Masonic</a> ticket. Incumbent <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Party" title="National Republican Party">National Republican</a> Governor <a href="/wiki/Levi_Lincoln,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Levi Lincoln, Jr.">Levi Lincoln, Jr.</a> was retiring so Adams faced that party's <a href="/wiki/John_Davis_(Massachusetts_governor)" title="John Davis (Massachusetts governor)">John Davis</a>, Democrat <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Morton" title="Marcus Morton">Marcus Morton</a> and Samuel L. Allen of the <a href="/wiki/Working_Men%27s_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Working Men's Party">Working Men's Party</a>. Davis won a plurality with 40%; Adams took 29%, with Morton taking 25% and Allen 6%. Because no candidate had won a majority, the election was decided by the state legislature. Adams withdrew and endorsed Davis, preferring him over Morton, and Davis was chosen in January 1834.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/James_Smithson" title="James Smithson">James Smithson</a> died and left his estate to the U.S. government to build an institution of learning, many in Congress wanted to use the money for other purposes. Adams played a key role ensuring that the money was instead used to build the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> In 1839, he was elected a member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Antiquarian_Society" title="American Antiquarian Society">American Antiquarian Society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adams opposed the <a href="/wiki/Texas_annexation" title="Texas annexation">annexation of Texas</a>, viewing as unconstitutional the imposition of U.S. citizenship on foreign nationals when those nationals did not hold a referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> Adams called for the annexation of the entirety of <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Country" title="Oregon Country">Oregon Country</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Oregon_boundary_dispute" title="Oregon boundary dispute">disputed region</a> occupied by both the United States and Britain, and was disappointed when President <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a> signed the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Treaty" title="Oregon Treaty">Oregon Treaty</a>, which divided the land between the two claimants at the 49th parallel.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> Adams became a strong critic of the <a href="/wiki/Mexican-American_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexican-American War">Mexican-American War</a>, which he saw as a war of aggression against <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> that was designed to take Mexican territory.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> Although the war was popular at first, many Whigs eventually opposed it.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although there is no indication that the two were close, Adams met <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> during the latter's sole term as a member of the House of Representatives, from 1847 until Adams' death.<sup id="cite_ref-unger_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unger-95">[94]</a></sup> Thus, it has been suggested that Adams is the only major figure in American history who knew both the <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers</a> and Abraham Lincoln; however, this is not so, as Martin Van Buren met Founding Fathers <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>, knew Founder <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a> (Van Buren's mentor), and met the young Lincoln while on a campaign trip through Illinois.<sup id="cite_ref-widmer_ted_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-widmer_ted-96">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams_and_abolitionism" title="John Quincy Adams and abolitionism">John Quincy Adams and abolitionism</a></div>
<p>A longtime opponent of slavery, Adams used his new role in Congress to fight it, and he became the most prominent national leader opposing slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> In 1836, Southern Representatives voted in a "<a href="/wiki/Gag_rule" title="Gag rule">gag rule</a>" that immediately tabled any petitions about slavery, thus preventing any discussion or debate of the slavery issue. He became a forceful opponent of this rule and conceived a way around it, attacking slavery in the House for two weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> The gag rule prevented him from bringing slavery petitions to the floor, but he brought one anyway. It was a petition from a Georgia citizen urging disunion due to the continuation of slavery in the South. Though he certainly did not support it and made that clear at the time, his intent was to antagonize the pro-slavery faction of Congress into an open fight on the matter.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> The plan worked.</p>
<p>The petition infuriated his Congressional enemies, many of whom were agitating for disunion themselves. They moved for his censure over the matter, enabling Adams to discuss slavery openly during his subsequent defense. Taking advantage of his right to defend himself, Adams delivered prepared and impromptu remarks against slavery and in favor of abolition.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> Knowing that he would probably be acquitted, he changed the focus from his own actions to those of the slaveholders, speaking against the slave trade and the ownership of slaves. He decided that if he were censured, he would merely resign, run for the office again, and probably win easily.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> When his opponents realized that they played into his political strategy, they tried to bury the censure. Adams made sure this did not happen, and the debate continued. He attacked slavery and slaveholders as immoral, and condemned the institution while calling for it to end.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> After two weeks, a vote was held, and he was not censured. He delighted in the misery he was inflicting on the slaveholders he so hated, and prided himself on being "obnoxious to the slave faction."<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the censure of Adams over the slavery petition was ultimately abandoned, the House did address the issue of petitions from enslaved persons at a later time. Adams again argued that the right to petition was a universal right, granted by God, so that those in the weakest positions might always have recourse to those in the most powerful. Adams also called into question the actions of a House that would limit its own ability to debate and resolve questions internally. After this debate, the gag rule was ultimately retained.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-98">[97]</a></sup> The discussion ignited by his actions and the attempts of others to quiet him raised questions of the right to petition, the right to legislative debate, and the morality of slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> During the censure debate, Adams said that he took delight in the thought that southerners would forever remember him as "the acutest, the astutest, the archest enemy of southern slavery that ever existed."<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1844, he chaired a committee for reform of the rules of Congress, and he used this opportunity to try once again to repeal the gag rule. He spent two months building support for this move, but due to northern opposition, the rule narrowly survived.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> He fiercely criticized northern Representatives and Senators, in particular <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas" title="Stephen A. Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a>, who seemed to cater to the slave faction in exchange for southern support.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> His opposition to slavery made him, along with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, one of the leading opponents of Texas annexation and the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a>. He correctly predicted that both would contribute to <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">civil war</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> After one of his reelection victories, he said that he must "bring about a day prophesied when slavery and war shall be banished from the face of the earth."<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> He wrote in his private journal in 1820:<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The discussion of this <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri question</a> has betrayed the secret of their souls. In the abstract they admit that slavery is an evil, they disclaim it, and cast it all upon the shoulder of…Great Britain. But when probed to the quick upon it, they show at the bottom of their souls pride and vainglory in their condition of masterdom. They look down upon the simplicity of a Yankee's manners, because he has no habits of overbearing like theirs and cannot treat negroes like dogs. It is among the evils of slavery that it taints the very sources of moral principle. It establishes false estimates of virtue and vice: for what can be more false and heartless than this doctrine which makes the first and holiest rights of humanity to depend upon the color of the skin?</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1841, at the request of <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Tappan" title="Lewis Tappan">Lewis Tappan</a> and Ellis Gray Loring, Adams joined the case of <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._The_Amistad" title="United States v. The Amistad">United States v. The Amistad</a></i>. Adams went before the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a> on behalf of African slaves who had revolted and seized the Spanish ship <i>Amistad</i>. Adams appeared on 24 February 1841, and spoke for four hours. His argument succeeded; the Court ruled in favor of the Africans, who were declared free and returned to their homes.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> Among his opponents was President <a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a>. In the following years, the Spanish government continued to press the US for compensation for the ship and its cargo, including the slaves. Several southern lawmakers introduced Congressional resolutions to appropriate money for such payment, but none passed, despite support from Democratic presidents <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Adams continued to speak out against what he called the "<a href="/wiki/Slave_Power" title="Slave Power">Slave Power</a>", that is the organized political power of the slave owners who dominated all the southern states and their representation in Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> He vehemently attacked the annexation of Texas (1845) and the Mexican War (1846–48) as part of a "conspiracy" to extend slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photography">Photography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Photography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sources contend that in 1843 Adams sat for the earliest confirmed photograph still in existence of a U.S. president, although others maintain that <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">William Henry Harrison</a> had posed even earlier for his portrait, in 1841.<sup id="cite_ref-whha-16_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whha-16-103">[102]</a></sup> The original <a href="/wiki/Daguerreotype" title="Daguerreotype">daguerreotype</a> is in the collection of the <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery_(United_States)" title="National Portrait Gallery (United States)">National Portrait Gallery</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nullification_crisis">Nullification crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Nullification crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Shortly after Adams entered Congress, the <a href="/wiki/Nullification_Crisis" title="Nullification Crisis">Nullification Crisis</a> threatened civil war over the <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1828" class="mw-redirect" title="Tariff of 1828">Tariff of 1828</a>. Adams offered an amendment moderating the tariff, and defused the crisis. Congress also passed the <a href="/wiki/Force_Bill" title="Force Bill">Force Bill</a> which authorized President <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> to use military force if Adams' compromise bill did not force the belligerent states to capitulate. There was no need, however, because Adams' compromise remedied the matter. The compromise actually did not alter the tariff as much as the southern states had hoped, though they agreed not to continue pursuing the issue for fear of civil war.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advancement_of_science">Advancement of science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Advancement of science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_(detail),_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_%28detail%29%2C_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG/170px-John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_%28detail%29%2C_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_%28detail%29%2C_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG/255px-John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_%28detail%29%2C_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_%28detail%29%2C_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG/340px-John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_%28detail%29%2C_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3240" data-file-height="4320" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_by_George_Caleb_Bingham_(detail),_c._1850_after_1844_original_-_DSC03235.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery_(United_States)" title="National Portrait Gallery (United States)">Adams' portrait at U.S. National Portrait Gallery</a> by <a href="/wiki/George_Caleb_Bingham" title="George Caleb Bingham">George Bingham</a> c. 1850</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adams also became a leading force for the promotion of science. As president, he had proposed a national observatory, which did not win much support. In 1829 British scientist <a href="/wiki/James_Smithson" title="James Smithson">James Smithson</a> died, and left his fortune for the "increase and diffusion of knowledge." In Smithson's will, he stated that should his nephew, Henry James Hungerford, die without heirs, the Smithson estate would go to the government of the United States to create an "Establishment for the increase &amp; diffusion of Knowledge among men." After the nephew died without heirs in 1835, President <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> informed <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> of the bequest, which amounted to about <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>500,000 ($75,000,000 in 2008 U.S. dollars after inflation). Adams realized that this might allow the United States to realize his dream of building a national institution of science and learning. Adams thus became Congress' primary supporter of the future <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The money was invested in shaky state bonds, which quickly defaulted. After heated debate in Congress, Adams successfully argued to restore the lost funds with interest.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> Though Congress wanted to use the money for other purposes, Adams successfully persuaded Congress to preserve the money for an institution of science and learning.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> Congress also debated whether the federal government had the authority to accept the gift, though with Adams leading the initiative, Congress decided to accept the legacy bequeathed to the nation and pledged the faith of the United States to the charitable trust on July 1, 1836.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>He also relentlessly pursued support for astronomical efforts and observatories, seeking a national observatory for the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> In 1825 Adams signed a bill for the creation of a national observatory just before leaving presidential office. His efforts led to the founding in 1830 of what is now the oldest, still-operational scientific institution of the United States, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Observatory" title="United States Naval Observatory">United States Naval Observatory</a>. Adams in fact spent many nights at the Observatory, with celebrated national astronomer and oceanographer <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Fontaine_Maury" title="Matthew Fontaine Maury">Matthew Fontaine Maury</a>, watching and charting the stars, which had always been one of Adams' avocations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG/220px-JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG/330px-JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG/440px-JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3901" data-file-height="2925" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JQAdams_QRc_CC.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Adams's <a href="/wiki/Cenotaph" title="Cenotaph">cenotaph</a> at the Congressional Cemetery</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1846, the 78-year-old former president suffered a stroke that left him partially paralyzed. After a few months of rest, he made a full recovery and resumed his duties in Congress. When Adams entered the House chamber, everyone "stood up and applauded."<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> On February 21, 1848, the House of Representatives was discussing the matter of honoring U.S. Army officers who served in the Mexican–American War. Adams had been a vehement critic of the war, and as Congressmen rose up to say, "Aye!" in favor of the measure, he instead yelled, "No!"<sup id="cite_ref-parker-1848_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parker-1848-109">[108]</a></sup> He rose to answer a question put forth by Speaker of the House <a href="/wiki/Robert_Charles_Winthrop" title="Robert Charles Winthrop">Robert Charles Winthrop</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-donaldson_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-donaldson-110">[109]</a></sup> Immediately thereafter, Adams collapsed, having suffered a massive <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_hemorrhage" class="mw-redirect" title="Cerebral hemorrhage">cerebral hemorrhage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-widmer-2008_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-widmer-2008-111">[110]</a></sup> Two days later, on February 23, he died with his wife and youngest son at his side in the Speaker's Room inside the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">Capitol Building</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> His last words were "This is the last of earth. I am content." He died at 7:20&#160;p.m.<sup id="cite_ref-donaldson_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-donaldson-110">[109]</a></sup> He was the last surviving child of John Adams. First term Representative <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> of Illinois was assigned to the committee making the funeral arrangements.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pohl_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pohl-113">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>His original interment was temporary, in the public vault at the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Cemetery" title="Congressional Cemetery">Congressional Cemetery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> Later, he was interred in the family burial ground in Quincy, Massachusetts, across from the First Parish Church, called <a href="/wiki/Hancock_Cemetery" title="Hancock Cemetery">Hancock Cemetery</a>. After Louisa's death in 1852, his son <a href="/wiki/Charles_Francis_Adams_Sr." title="Charles Francis Adams Sr.">Charles Francis Adams</a> had his parents reinterred in the expanded family crypt in the <a href="/wiki/United_First_Parish_Church,_Quincy,_Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect" title="United First Parish Church, Quincy, Massachusetts">United First Parish Church</a> across the street, next to John and Abigail. Both tombs are viewable by the public. Adams' original tomb at Hancock Cemetery is still there and marked simply "J.Q. Adams".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JQ_Adams_Vault,_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/82/JQ_Adams_Vault%2C_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg/220px-JQ_Adams_Vault%2C_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/82/JQ_Adams_Vault%2C_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg/330px-JQ_Adams_Vault%2C_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/82/JQ_Adams_Vault%2C_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg/440px-JQ_Adams_Vault%2C_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JQ_Adams_Vault,_Hancock_Cemetery.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Quincy Adams' original tomb at <a href="/wiki/Hancock_Cemetery" title="Hancock Cemetery">Hancock Cemetery</a>, across the street from United First Parish Church</div>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Adams and Louisa had three sons and a daughter. Their daughter, Louisa, was born in 1811 but died in 1812 while the family was in Russia. They named their first son <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Adams" title="George Washington Adams">George Washington Adams</a> (1801–1829) after the first president. Both George and their second son, <a href="/wiki/John_Adams_II" title="John Adams II">John</a> (1803–1834), led troubled lives and died in early adulthood.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> (George committed suicide and John was expelled from Harvard before his 1823 graduation.)</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">

</li>

</div>

</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Adams' youngest son, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Francis_Adams_Sr." title="Charles Francis Adams Sr.">Charles Francis Adams</a> (who named his own son <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams_II" title="John Quincy Adams II">John Quincy</a>), pursued a career in diplomacy and politics. In 1870 Charles Francis built the first <a href="/wiki/Presidential_library" title="Presidential library">presidential library</a> in the United States, to honor his father. The <a href="/wiki/Adams_National_Historical_Park#Stone_Library" title="Adams National Historical Park">Stone Library</a> includes over 14,000 books written in twelve languages. The library is located in the "<a href="/wiki/Peacefield" title="Peacefield">Old House</a>" at <a href="/wiki/Adams_National_Historical_Park" title="Adams National Historical Park">Adams National Historical Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Quincy,_Massachusetts" title="Quincy, Massachusetts">Quincy, Massachusetts</a>.</p>
<p>John Adams and John Quincy Adams were the only father and son to serve as presidents until <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> (1989–1993) and <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> (2001–2009).</p>
<p>It has been suggested that John Quincy Adams had the highest I.Q. of any U.S. president.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Personality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_$1_Coin_obverse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg/150px-John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg" width="150" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg/225px-John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg/300px-John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1098" data-file-height="1098" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Quincy_Adams_Presidential_$1_Coin_obverse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Presidential_Dollar" class="mw-redirect" title="Presidential Dollar">Presidential Dollar</a> of John Quincy Adams</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adams' personality was much like that of his father, as were his political beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> He always preferred secluded reading to social engagements, and several times had to be pressured by others to remain in public service. Historian Paul Nagel states that, like <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> after him, Adams often suffered from depression, for which he sought some form of treatment in early years. Adams thought his depression was due to the high expectations demanded of him by his father and mother. Throughout his life he felt inadequate and socially awkward because of his depression, and was constantly bothered by his physical appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> He was closer to his father, whom he spent much of his early life with abroad, than he was to his mother. When he was younger and the American Revolution was going on, his mother told her children what their father was doing, and what he was risking, and because of this Adams grew to greatly respect his father.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> His relationship with his mother was rocky; she had high expectations of him and was afraid her children might end up dead alcoholics like her brother.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> His biographer, Nagel, concludes that his mother's disapproval of Louisa Johnson motivated him to marry Johnson in 1797, despite Adams' reservations that Johnson, like his mother, had a strong personality.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Graves_of_the_Adams,_Quincy,_Massachusetts.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG/220px-Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG/330px-Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG/440px-Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Graves_of_the_Adams,_Quincy,_Massachusetts.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tombs of Presidents <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> (left) and John Quincy Adams (right) and their wives, in a family crypt beneath the <a href="/wiki/United_First_Parish_Church,_Quincy,_Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect" title="United First Parish Church, Quincy, Massachusetts">United First Parish Church</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Though he later described his presidency as the unhappiest time of his life,<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup> scholars rate John Quincy Adams in the second quartile in the majority of <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of presidents of the United States">historical presidential rankings</a>. Historians have often included Adams among the leading conservatives of his day.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagel_1999_6-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel_1999-6">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mattie_2003_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mattie_2003-118">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Russell_Kirk" title="Russell Kirk">Russell Kirk</a>, however, sees Adams as a flawed conservative who was imprudent in opposing slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-Mattie_2003_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mattie_2003-118">[117]</a></sup> Adams' foreign policy legacy, and its focus on noninterventionism, led to his name being adopted by the John Quincy Adams Society, a network of student groups that is "committed to identifying, educating, and equipping the next generation of scholars and policy leaders to encourage a new era of realism and restraint in American foreign policy."<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams_Birthplace" title="John Quincy Adams Birthplace">John Quincy Adams Birthplace</a> is now part of <a href="/wiki/Adams_National_Historical_Park" title="Adams National Historical Park">Adams National Historical Park</a> and open to the public. <a href="/wiki/Adams_House_(Harvard_University)" class="mw-redirect" title="Adams House (Harvard University)">Adams House</a>, one of twelve undergraduate residential Houses at Harvard University, is named in honor of John Adams, John Quincy Adams, and other members of the Adams family who were associated with Harvard.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> The name Quincy has been used by several locations in the United States, including the town of <a href="/wiki/Quincy,_Illinois" title="Quincy, Illinois">Quincy, Illinois</a>. <a href="/wiki/Adams_County,_Illinois" title="Adams County, Illinois">Adams County, Illinois</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adams_County,_Indiana" title="Adams County, Indiana">Adams County, Indiana</a> are also named after Adams.</p>
<p>He was the first president to adopt a short haircut instead of long hair tied in a queue and to regularly wear long <a href="/wiki/Trousers" title="Trousers">trousers</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Culottes" title="Culottes">knee breeches</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_and_television">Film and television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Film and television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Adams occasionally is featured in the mass media. In the <a href="/wiki/Public_Broadcasting_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="Public Broadcasting Service">PBS</a> miniseries <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adams_Chronicles" title="The Adams Chronicles">The Adams Chronicles</a></i> (1976), he was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/David_Birney" title="David Birney">David Birney</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Daniels" title="William Daniels">William Daniels</a>, Marcel Trenchard, Steven Grover and Mark Winkworth. He was also portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Hopkins" title="Anthony Hopkins">Anthony Hopkins</a> in the 1997 film <i><a href="/wiki/Amistad_(film)" title="Amistad (film)">Amistad</a></i>, and again by <a href="/wiki/Ebon_Moss-Bachrach" title="Ebon Moss-Bachrach">Ebon Moss-Bachrach</a> and Steven Hinkle in the 2008 <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">HBO</a> television miniseries <i><a href="/wiki/John_Adams_(TV_miniseries)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Adams (TV miniseries)">John Adams</a></i>; the HBO series received criticism for needless historical and temporal distortions in its portrayal.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup></p>
<table class="collapsible collapsed" style="margin: 0.3em auto auto; clear:none; min-width:100%; width:100%; font-size:88%; border:1px solid #aaa;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronunciation_note">Pronunciation note</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Quincy_Adams&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Pronunciation note">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Quincy family name was pronounced <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>, as in the name of <a href="/wiki/Quincy,_Massachusetts" title="Quincy, Massachusetts">Quincy, Massachusetts</a>, where Adams was born. All of the other Quincy place names are locally <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>. Though not accurate, this pronunciation is also commonly used for Adams' middle name.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
